الإسلام والعلوم العقلية
والتابعين، وهو جو نظيف بلا نزاع ، وهو الذي ينبغي أن يكون»، ولكن الذي قد وقع - بخوض الخائضين من أهل الملل والنحل والفرق المبتدعة ـ غير الذي تمناه وكلنا نتمناه، فالكلام عن ممارسة العلوم العقلية يأتي في هذا الجو المتجدد على رغم إرادتنا واختيارنا ، فهل نُخْفِي رؤوسنا في الرمال كالنعامة، ظانين أنه لا إشكال أصلا ، أم نفتح باب هذه العلوم الخطيرة أمام كل طارق يطرقه ، أم هل هناك رأي آخر أسَدُّ منهما ؟
يقول الإمام تاج الدين السبكي رَحِمَهُ اللهُ مشيرا إلى جواب هذا السؤال: «أما المنطق فـ....إنه حرام على من لم تَرْسُخْ قواعد الشريعة في قلبه، ويمتلئ جوفه من عظمة هذا النبي الكريم وشرعته ، ويحفظ الكتاب العزيز ، وشيئا كثيرا من حديث النبي صل اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ على طريقة المحدثين، ويعرف من فروع الفقه يسمى به فقيها، مفتيا مشارا إليه من أهل مذهبه ... وأما من وصل إلى هذا المقام فله النظر فيها ؛ للرد على أهلها ولكن بشرطين: أحدهما أن يثق من نفسه بأنه وصل إلى درجة لا تزعزعها رياحُ الأباطيل ، وشبه الأضاليل، وأهواء الملاحدة. والثاني ألا يمزج كلامهم بكلام علماء الإسلام، فلقد حصل ضرر عظيم على المسلمين بمزج كلام الحكماء بكلام المتكلمين، وأدى الحال إلى طعن المشبهة وغيرهم من رعاع الخلق في أصحابنا، وما كان ذلك إلا في زماننا وقبله بيسير، منذ نشأ نصير الدين الطوسي ومن تبعه، لا حياهم الله . فإن قلت: فقد خاض حجة الإسلام الغزالي، والإمام فخر الدين الرازي
في علوم الفلسفة ودونوها، وخلطوها بكلام المتكلمين، فهلا تنكر عليهما ؟ قلت: إن هذين إمامان جليلان ، ولم يَخُضن واحد منهما في هذه العلوم حتى صار قدوة في الدين، وضُرِبت الأمثالُ باسمهما في معرفة علم الكلام
على طريقة أهل السنة والجماعة ، من الصحابة والتابعين فمن بعدهم، فإياك أن تسمع شيئا غير ذلك ، فتضل ضلالا مبينا فهذان إمامان عظيمان، وكان حقا عليهما نصر المؤمنين ، وإعزاز هذا الدين بدفع ترَّهات أولئك المبطلين، فمن
وصل إلى مقامهما لا ملام عليه بالنظر في الكتب الفلسفية، بل هو مثاب مأجور . . . »(
وقد تصديت لهذه القضية متعمدا ؛ لتوضيح بعض الإشكاليات العويصة التي تمثل عُقْدَةً لا تنحل عند كثير من أهل العلم، فضلا عن العوام. وتأتي الإشكالية من جديد حين نقبل على دراسة الحالة العلمية والفكرية في الهند؛ حيث إن المنهج الفكري السائد في الهند وإن كان هو الفكر الأشعري الماتريدي عموما، إلا أنه قد امتزجت به الأفكار الفلسفية، المشائية وغير المشائية، وهذا الامتزاج والخلط واضح كل الوضوح في مؤلفات كثير من علماء الهند في العلوم العقلية، بحيث يتحير الناظر فيه إن لم يكن على بصيرة علمية، فصار لزاما علينا التعرض لهذه النقطة.
ولا أريد الخوض في هذا المضيق؛ لأن المقام ليس مقام تفصيل وإطناب، بالإضافة إلى أني قد أشرت قبلا إلى بعض النقاط التي تتعلق بهذا المبحث في مستهل حديثي . فالذي أردت هنا هو مجرد تأكيد لحقيقة، هي أن المنهج الأشعري الذي تلقاه الأمة بالقبول هو المنهج الصحيح الذي لم يحدث فيه أي انحراف، لا في منهجه ولا في معتقداته. وظاهرة الخلط بين العلوم الفلسفية في كتبنا الكلامية والأصولية، ومساهمة أئمتنا في تلك العلوم الأجنبية تأليفا وتعليما هي مما اتهم به المذهب من قِبل المجسمة، كما أشار إليه التاج
Islam and the intellectual sciences. And the followers, and it is a pure atmosphere without dispute, and that is what should be. But what has occurred - due to the plunging of those who plunge from the people of creeds, sects, and innovated factions - is other than what he wished, and all of us wish it. So speech about practicing the intellectual sciences comes in this renewed atmosphere despite our will and choice. So do we hide our heads in the sand like an ostrich, thinking there is no problem at all? Or do we open the door of these serious sciences to every knocker who knocks on it? Or is there another opinion more sound than these two? Imam Taj al-Din al-Subki, may Allah have mercy on him, says, indicating the answer to this question: 'As for logic... it is forbidden for one whose foundations of Sharia have not become firmly rooted in his heart, and whose innermost being is filled with the greatness of this noble Prophet and his Law, and who has memorized the Mighty Book and a great deal of the hadith of the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, according to the method of the hadith scholars, and who knows from the branches of jurisprudence what earns him the title of jurist, mufti, and a reference among the people of his school... But as for one who has reached this station, he may examine it in order to refute its people, but with two conditions: one is that he be confident in himself that he has reached a degree that cannot be shaken by the winds of falsehoods, the semblances of misguidance, and the desires of the atheists. The second is that he does not mix their speech with the speech of the scholars of Islam, for great harm has befallen the Muslims due to mixing the speech of the philosophers with the speech of the theologians, and the situation led to the slander of the anthropomorphists and other riffraff of creation against our companions, and that only happened in our time and shortly before it, since the emergence of Nasir al-Din al-Tusi and those who followed him, may Allah not give them life. If you say: 'But the Proof of Islam al-Ghazali and Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi delved into the sciences of philosophy and wrote about them, and mixed them with the speech of the theologians, so why do you not denounce them?' I say: 'These two are eminent imams, and neither of them delved into these sciences until he had become a model in religion, and proverbs were coined about them in the knowledge of theology according to the way of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, from the Companions and Followers and those after them. So beware of hearing anything other than that, lest you go astray with a manifest straying. So these two are great imams, and it was incumbent upon them to support the believers and strengthen this religion by repelling the nonsense of those falsifiers. So whoever reaches their station is not to be blamed for examining philosophical books; rather, he is rewarded and recompensed...' I have deliberately taken up this issue to clarify some of the knotty problems that represent a complex that is not untied for many scholars, let alone the common people. The problem arises anew when we approach the study of the scientific and intellectual situation in India; for although the prevailing intellectual method in India is generally Ash'ari-Maturidi thought, yet philosophical ideas, Peripatetic and otherwise, have become mixed with it, and this mixing and blending is very clear in the writings of many Indian scholars in the intellectual sciences, to the extent that the observer is perplexed unless he has scientific insight. So it became necessary for us to address this point. I do not wish to delve into this narrow strait because the context is not one of detail and prolixity, besides which I have already pointed to some points related to this topic at the beginning of my speech. What I intended here is merely to affirm a truth, which is that the Ash'ari method which the community has accepted is the correct method in which no deviation has occurred, neither in its method nor in its beliefs. The phenomenon of mixing between the philosophical sciences in our theological and usul books, and the contribution of our imams to those foreign sciences in authorship and teaching, are among the things for which the school has been accused by the anthropomorphists, as al-Taj referred to.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a front-matter discussion by the author of Bahr Ulum, addressing the controversial issue of studying logic and philosophical sciences. It opens by acknowledging the ideal of a pure Islamic environment free from innovation, but then states that reality has diverged due to the involvement of people from various sects. The author poses three options: ignore the problem, open the door indiscriminately, or find a middle path. He then presents the answer of Taj al-Din al-Subki, who states that logic is forbidden for those not firmly grounded in Sharia, but permissible for one who has reached a high level of religious knowledge and confidence, under two conditions: (1) certainty that his faith cannot be shaken by doubts, and (2) not mixing philosophical speech with Islamic scholarship. Al-Subki warns that mixing has caused great harm, leading to criticisms of Ash'ari scholars by anthropomorphists, especially since Nasir al-Din al-Tusi. The author then anticipates an objection: al-Ghazali and al-Razi themselves mixed philosophy with theology. The response is that these two imams were exceptions—they had reached such a high station in religion that their engagement was for defense of the faith, and they are rewarded for it. The author then turns to the situation in India, where Ash'ari-Maturidi thought is mixed with Greek and other philosophies, causing confusion. He reaffirms that the authentic Ash'ari method is free from deviation, and that the mixing seen in some books is not a defect of the method itself but part of historical circumstances. The passage serves as a methodological justification for studying logic and philosophy under strict conditions, and it sets the tone for the rest of the work.

Key Terms
المنطق
الشريعة
المتكلمون
الفلسفة

D) Being a mujtahid in fiqh.

Key Takeaway
The study of logic is not unconditionally permissible; it requires deep religious grounding and the intention to refute falsehood, as exemplified by al-Ghazali and al-Razi.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
Defending Ash'ari Methodology Against Philosophical Deviations
Author defends Ash'ari methodology, warns against philosophy mixing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's core thesis about Ash'ari methodology and distinguish his stance from both blind rejection and uncritical acceptance of philosophy.

Core Idea
Authentic Ash'ari methodology is correct; philosophical mixing must be critically examined, not blindly followed.

In this opening passage, the author of Bahr al-Ulum establishes his methodological stance. He warns against deviation from the path of Ahl al-Sunna, but also distinguishes between harmful philosophy and beneficial engagement when done from a position of strength. He addresses the specific problem of philosophical mixing in Indian scholarly works and defends the Ash'ari tradition against accusations from anthropomorphists. Understanding his position will help you navigate tensions between tradition and rational inquiry in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim about the Ash'ari path
Locate where the author states that Ash'ari methodology is the correct path of Ahl al-Sunna. Notice his emphatic warning against hearing anything else.
This is his foundational premise.
2
Locate the problem of mixing in India
Find the paragraph where he discusses the intellectual situation in India. Note that Ash'ari-Maturidi thought is mixed with Peripatetic and other philosophies, causing confusion.
The author sees this as a problem requiring clarification.
3
Distinguish his reference imams from others
He names al-Ghazali and al-Subki as model imams who combined fiqh, theology, logic, and Sufism. Contrast with al-Nawawi and Ibn al-Salah, whose hostile stance toward logic he interprets differently.
This shows his nuanced view: not all anti-philosophy scholars are his reference.
4
Analyze al-Subki's warning against Peripatetics
Read the quoted warning. Al-Subki calls al-Farabi and Ibn Sina 'fools' for labeling their falsehoods as Islamic wisdom. The author endorses this critique.
This is his evidence that the school has not deviated.

مجرد تأكيد لحقيقة، هي أن المنهج الأشعري الذي تلقاه الأمة بالقبول هو المنهج الصحيح الذي لم يحدث فيه أي انحراف، لا في منهجه ولا في معتقداته. وظاهرة الخلط بين العلوم الفلسفية في كتبنا الكلامية والأصولية، ومساهمة أئمتنا في تلك العلوم الأجنبية تأليفا وتعليما هي مما اتهم به المذهب من قِبل المجسمة، كما أشار إليه التاج السبكي فيما نقلناه عنه آنفا، ولا يزال أذنابهم يرموننا بالعظائم بهذا السبب، من ذلك شبكة Explanation of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum according to the way of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, from the Companions and the Followers and those after them. So beware of hearing anything other than that, lest you go astray into clear misguidance. These two are great imams, and it was right upon them to support the believers and strengthen this religion by repelling the nonsense of those false claimants. Whoever reaches their station is not blamed for looking into philosophical books; rather he is rewarded and recompensed. And I have undertaken this issue deliberately to clarify some of the abstruse problems that represent a knot not untied by many scholars, let alone the common people. The problem arises anew when we approach the study of the scientific and intellectual situation in India; for although the prevalent intellectual methodology in India is generally Ash'ari-Maturidi thought, it has been mixed with philosophical ideas, Peripatetic and non-Peripatetic. This mixing and confusion is very clear in the works of many Indian scholars in the rational sciences, such that the observer is perplexed unless he possesses scientific insight. So it became incumbent upon us to address this point. I do not wish to delve into this narrow strait because the place is not a place for detail and elaboration, besides which I have already hinted at some points related to this topic at the beginning of my speech. What I intended here is merely an affirmation of a truth, which is that the Ash'ari methodology, which the community has accepted, is the correct methodology in which no deviation has occurred, neither in its method nor in its beliefs. The phenomenon of mixing philosophical sciences in our theological and foundational books, and the contribution of our imams to those foreign sciences in authorship and teaching, is something for which the school has been accused by the anthropomorphists, as al-Taj al-Subki indicated in what we quoted from him earlier. Their followers still accuse us of great sins for this reason, among which is a net by which they catch weak souls. Our scholars in India have a footing, and what a footing, in these rational sciences. How then, in light of these facts, can we prove that the school has not deviated from the path and has not strayed from correctness? Nor have we been unmindful of our imams' takfir of those who say the world is eternal and deny God's knowledge of particulars, and we do not hesitate in the correctness of this judgment at all; it is the correct view that no other is acceptable. By imams here I do not mean those like al-Nawawi and Ibn al-Salah, known for their hostile stance toward logic and philosophy, for their words have an interpretation befitting their rank; rather I mean by them our theologian imams, most notably Hujjat al-Islam al-Ghazali and Taj al-Din al-Subki and their likes. They were jurists, theologians, logicians, and Sufis; their statement is the mainstay of Ahl al-Sunna in principles, and they are the ones who warned us against these false statements and their proponents. Among them is a group; al-Taj al-Subki says, warning against the Peripatetic philosophers: 'Follow the way of Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Abu Ali Ibn Sina and other philosophers who grew up in this umma and occupied themselves with their falsehoods and ignorances, calling it Islamic wisdom, and titled themselves the wise ones of Islam, though they are more worthy to be called fools.'
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is an introductory and methodological exposition by the author of Bahr al-Ulum. He begins by affirming that his commentary follows the path of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, warning against deviation. He praises two great imams (likely al-Ghazali and al-Subki) for defending the religion against falsehoods. He then explains his purpose: to clarify certain abstruse issues that puzzle many scholars. The specific context is the mixing of Ash'ari-Maturidi thought with philosophical ideas (especially Peripatetic) in India. This mixing causes confusion in rational sciences. The author stresses that the authentic Ash'ari methodology is correct and free from deviation. He acknowledges that the mixing of philosophical sciences in theological books has been used by anthropomorphists (al-Mujassima) to attack the school. He cites Taj al-Din al-Subki's warning against the Peripatetic philosophers (al-Farabi, Ibn Sina) who called their falsehood 'Islamic wisdom' but are really fools. The author also clarifies that he does not include imams like al-Nawawi and Ibn al-Salah in his praise because their hostile stance toward logic has a different interpretation; rather he refers to theologians like al-Ghazali and al-Subki who combined fiqh, theology, logic, and Sufism. The passage serves to establish the author's methodological stance: he is defending the Ash'ari tradition while critically engaging with philosophy, but distinguishing between beneficial engagement and blind acceptance. The key point is that the Ash'ari methodology as accepted by the community is the correct path, and any mixing with philosophy must be critically examined without deviating from core beliefs.

المشائية

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Sunni Scholars and the Rational Sciences
Author justifies Sunni scholars' study of rational sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the defensive rationale behind Sunni theologians' deep engagement with logic and philosophy, citing specific scholars and their purpose.

Core Idea
Ash'ari scholars studied logic and philosophy not to adopt them, but to dismantle false arguments.

This passage addresses a recurring question: if logic and philosophy were criticized by some Sunni scholars, why did others like al-Ghazali and al-Razi become experts in them? The author clarifies that these imams used rational sciences as tools for refuting heretical doctrines, not as ends in themselves. Understanding this context is essential for appreciating the role of logic in Islamic intellectual history.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scholarly stances
Locate in the passage the group of scholars who were hostile to logic (e.g., al-Nawawi, Ibn al-Salah) and the group who engaged deeply (e.g., al-Ghazali, al-Razi, al-Dawani). Note the difference in their approaches.
The author sets aside the hostile group, saying their words have 'an interpretation befitting their rank.'
2
Extract the purpose of deep engagement
Read the author's explanation: 'those who took on the task of refuting opponents in creed needed to study the sciences of opponents.' The purpose is defense, not adoption.
The author mentions that they studied 'with depth and expansion' to become experts in refutation.
3
Recognize the potential negative side effect
The author acknowledges that such immersion can lead to negative effects, advising caution despite the scholars' greatness.
This shows a balanced assessment: the tool is necessary but carries risks.
4
Connect to the broader Ash'ari tradition
The passage links this practice to a line of Ash'ari theologians from al-Ghazali to al-Subki and the Indian scholars, all united on the Ash'ari creed while using rational sciences as instruments.
The author stresses that they are 'one hand on one creed' despite diverse specializations.

الإسلام والعلوم العقلية
السبكي فيما نقلناه عنه آنفا، ولا يزال أذنابهم يرموننا بالعظائم بهذا السبب، من ذلك شبكة يصطادون بها ضعاف النفوس، وعلمائنا في الهند لهم قَدَمُ أيُّ قدم في هذه العلوم العقلية، فكيف نثبت في ضوء هذه الحقائق أن المذهب لم ينحرف عن الجادة، ولم يضل عن الصواب ؟
ولم يَغِب عن بالنا تكفير أئمتنا لمن يقول بقدم العالم وينفي علم الله بالجزئيات، ولا نتردد في صحة هذا الحكم أبدا ، وهو الصحيح الذي لا يُقبل غيره، ولستُ أعني بالأئمة هنا أمثال الإمامين النووي وابن الصلاح، المعروف عنهم موقفهم العدائي للمنطق والفلسفة ؛ لأن لكلامهم تأويلا يليق بمقامهم ولكني أعني بهم أئمتنا المتكلمين من أبرزهم حجة الإسلام الغزالي وتاج الدين السبكي وأمثالهما، كانوا فقهاء ومتكلمين ومناطقة وصوفية، على قولهم معول أهل السنة في الأصول، وهم الذين حذرونا من هذه الأقوال الباطلة وقائليها.
(1)
ومنهم طائفة يقول التاج السبكي محذرا من الفلاسفة المشائية: ... تبعث طريقة أبي نصر الفارابي وأبي على ابن سينا وغيرهما من الفلاسفة ، الذين نشئوا في هذه الأمة، واشتغلوا بأباطيلهم وجهالاتهم، وسموها الحكمة الإسلامية، ولقبوا أنفسهم حكماء الإسلام ، وهم أحق بأن يسموا سفهاء جهلاء من أن يسموا حكماء ... » .
ثم يأتي طائفة من العلماء في الهند، يُحسبون من الأشاعرة أو الحنفية، ولكننا نجد عندهم منهجا مختلفا عما قرره التاج السبكي، وهو في الحقيقة لا يخص علماء الهند فقط ، بل هناك أئمة أشاعرة في الخارج، سبقوهم في هذا المجال، كالرازي والبيضاوي والإيجي والسعد والسيد الشريف والمحقق الدواني وآخرون، بل والغزالي نفسه، وكأن الهنود تبع لهم في هذه الصنعة. ولا شك أنهم كبار ، ولا يمكن اعتبار أقوالهم ومواقفهم ببساطة، فالحاصل أن النقد والتقييد لا بد أن يسبقهما دراسة ودراسة قراءة وقراءة، قبل الهجوم على التقول ، وكيف نُبَرِّر هذه الظاهرة ؟
وموجز القول في حلها: أن علماء السنة على اختلاف تخصصاتهم واتجاهاتهم يد واحدة، على عقيدة واحدة، عقيدة الإمام أبي الحسن الأشعري، منهم من تصدى للرد على المخالفين في العقيدة، فيحتاجون إلى دراسة ما عند المخالفين من العلوم فيدرسونها بعمق ويتوسعون فيها، حتى يعد إماما في فنهم ، ، كما حدث للغزالي والرازي والسيد والسعد والدواني ... لكثرة تداولهم لهذه العلوم، واختلاطهم بشبهاتهم، وتبحرهم في تشكيكاتهم، إلى حد أن الفلسفة السيناوية وتراث اليونان العقلي بدأت تُفهم من خلال شروحهم وبيانهم ، وبدأ المتظاهرون بالفلسفة يتتلمذون لأئمة علم الكلام في فهم الفلسفة وهذا ، وإن كان فيه ما يدل على عظمة أئمتنا واستنارتهم العقلية، إلا أنه قد يؤدي إلى آثار سلبية أحيانا ، مهما كان الإنسان على حذر، أو
Islam and the Rational Sciences
Al-Subki, as we quoted him earlier, and his followers still accuse us of great things because of this, including a net with which they catch weak souls. Our scholars in India have a firm footing in these rational sciences. So how can we affirm, in light of these truths, that the school [of thought] has not deviated from the path nor strayed from correctness?
Nor have we forgotten the takfir of our imams against those who say the world is eternal and deny God's knowledge of particulars, and we do not hesitate in the validity of this judgment at all. It is the correct one which no other is accepted. By 'imams' here I do not mean figures like al-Nawawi and Ibn al-Salah, known for their hostile stance towards logic and philosophy, because their words have an interpretation befitting their rank. Rather, I mean our theological imams, foremost among them Hujjat al-Islam al-Ghazali, Taj al-Din al-Subki, and their likes. They were jurists, theologians, logicians, and Sufis, and their statement is the relied-upon of the Ahl al-Sunna in fundamentals. They are the ones who warned us against these false statements and their proponents.
(1) Among them is a group: Taj al-Subki says, warning against the Peripatetic philosophers: 'The method of Abu Nasr al-Farabi, Abu Ali ibn Sina, and other philosophers who arose in this nation and occupied themselves with their falsehoods and ignorances, calling it Islamic wisdom, and titled themselves the sages of Islam, while they are more deserving of being called foolish ignoramuses than being called sages...'
Then comes a group of scholars in India, counted as Ash'aris or Hanafis, but we find with them a methodology different from what Taj al-Subki established. In reality, it is not specific to Indian scholars; rather, there are Ash'ari imams abroad who preceded them in this field, such as al-Razi, al-Baydawi, al-Iji, al-Sa'd, al-Sayyid al-Sharif, al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani, and others, even al-Ghazali himself. It is as if the Indians were followers of them in this craft. Undoubtedly, they are great, and their statements and stances cannot be taken lightly. The conclusion is that criticism and restriction must be preceded by study and study, reading and reading, before attacking claims. So how do we justify this phenomenon?
The summary of the solution is: The Sunni scholars, despite their different specializations and orientations, are united on one creed, the creed of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. Among them are those who took on the task of refuting opponents in creed, so they needed to study the sciences of opponents; they studied them deeply and expanded in them, to the point that one became an imam in that discipline. This happened with al-Ghazali, al-Razi, al-Sayyid, al-Sa'd, al-Dawani, etc., due to their frequent handling of these sciences, their mixing with the opponents' doubts, and their deep immersion in their criticisms, to the extent that Avicennan philosophy and the Greek intellectual heritage came to be understood through their commentaries and expositions. Those who pretended to be philosophers began to study under the imams of kalam to understand philosophy. This, although it indicates the greatness of our imams and their intellectual enlightenment, may sometimes lead to negative effects, however cautious one may be.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a methodological reflection defending the study of rational sciences (logic and philosophy) by prominent Sunni scholars. The author begins by acknowledging the criticism that some Muslims face for engaging with these disciplines. He specifically mentions the hostility of figures like al-Nawawi and Ibn al-Salah toward logic and philosophy, but sets them aside, focusing instead on major Ash'ari theologians like al-Ghazali and Taj al-Din al-Subki, who were also logicians and Sufis. These figures, he argues, are the true authorities and they warned against false doctrines. He then contrasts two groups: one that warns against philosophers (citing al-Subki's condemnation of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina) and another that engages deeply with these sciences for the purpose of refutation. This second group includes not only Indian scholars but also earlier Ash'ari theologians like al-Razi and al-Dawani. The author explains that these scholars studied logic and philosophy not as ends in themselves, but as tools to defend the creed against opponents. They became so proficient that even self-proclaimed philosophers learned from them. The author acknowledges that such deep engagement can have negative side effects, but emphasizes that it was necessary for defense.

Key Terms
العلوم العقلية
الفلاسفة المشائية
تكفير

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) To oppose Imam al-Nawawi.
D) To establish a new philosophical school.

Key Takeaway
Sunni scholars engaged deeply with rational sciences not as an end, but as a weapon to defend the creed against heretical innovations.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Sunni Scholars and Rational Sciences
Author discusses historical reasons for Sunni scholars studying philosophy and logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the reasons Sunni scholars studied rational sciences and the risks they identified, using the passage from Bahr al-Ulum.

Core Idea
Sunni scholars mastered philosophy to refute it, not to adopt it.

This passage from Bahr al-Ulum discusses why many Sunni scholars, especially Ash'aris, delved into philosophy and logic. The author explains that this was a strategic necessity for defending the creed, but he also warns of spiritual dangers. Understanding this background helps us appreciate how the Islamic intellectual tradition engaged with foreign sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phenomenon
Note that the author mentions Indian scholars and earlier Ash'ari imams like al-Razi and al-Ghazali who studied rational sciences. What is the common pattern?
They all engaged deeply with opponents' sciences.
2
Extract the justification
Find the sentence that gives the reason: 'they needed to study the sciences of the opponents in order to refute them'. This is the core justification.
The purpose is defensive, not acceptance.
3
Recognize the risks
The author uses an analogy of a soldier who may be wounded. What does this teach about the potential negative effects of studying these sciences?
Even with good intentions, one can be affected.
4
Synthesize the argument
Combine the justification and risks: Sunni scholars engaged to prove superiority, but it requires caution and prior knowledge of Shari'a.
This explains why conditions were set for studying such sciences.

شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم

ثم يأتي طائفة من العلماء ء في الهند، يُحسبون من الأشاعرة أو الحنفية، ولكننا نجد عندهم منهجا مختلفا عما قرره التاج السبكي، وهو في الحقيقة لا يخص علماء الهند فقط ، بل هناك أئمة أشاعرة في الخارج، سبقوهم في هذا المجال، كالرازي والبيضاوي والإيجي والسعد والسيد الشريف والمحقق الدواني وآخرون، بل والغزالي نفسه، وكأن الهنود تبع لهم في هذه الصنعة. ولا شك أنهم كبار ، ولا يمكن اعتبار أقوالهم ومواقفهم ببساطة، فالحاصل أن النقد والتقييد لا بد أن يسبقهما دراسة ودراسة قراءة وقراءة، قبل الهجوم على التقول ، وكيف نُبَرِّر هذه الظاهرة ؟

Commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum
Ignorant people who want to be called philosophers... (1).
Then a group of scholars in India came, considered among the Ash'aris or Hanafis, but we find with them a different methodology from what Taj al-Subki established. In reality, this is not specific to Indian scholars; rather, there are Ash'ari imams outside who preceded them in this field, such as al-Razi, al-Baydawi, al-Iji, al-Sa'd, al-Sayyid al-Sharif, al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani, and others, even al-Ghazali himself. It seems the Indians followed them in this craft. No doubt they are great, and their statements and positions cannot be taken lightly. The point is that criticism and restriction must be preceded by study and more study, reading and more reading, before attacking the [opposition's] discourse. How do we justify this phenomenon?
The summary of the solution is that Sunni scholars, despite their different specializations and orientations, are one hand upon one creed, the creed of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. Among them are those who undertook to refute opponents in creed, so they needed to study the sciences of the opponents. They studied them deeply and expanded in them, until they became imams in that art, as happened with al-Ghazali, al-Razi, al-Sayyid, al-Sa'd, al-Dawani... due to their frequent engagement with these sciences, their mixing with the opponents' doubts, and their deep immersion in their skepticisms, to the extent that Avicennan philosophy and the intellectual heritage of the Greeks began to be understood through their commentaries and explanations. Those who pretended to be philosophers began to study under the imams of kalam to understand philosophy. Although this indicates the greatness of our imams and their intellectual enlightenment, it may sometimes lead to negative effects (2), no matter how cautious one is, or armed with the weapon of Shari'a sciences, as we previously mentioned the imams' conditions for the student of these sciences.
Its example is like one who carries a weapon to fight the disbelievers, entering the thick of battle and fighting bravely, yet he may be struck by what Allah wills, and that one gets stained with blood. Rarely does he escape.
Whoever wants to prove the superiority of Ahl al-Sunna over others in all sciences, especially those sciences in which opponents take pride, boast, and become arrogant, and belittle us and mock us – like the philosophers, Mu'tazila, chemists, physicists, and others – a group of Sunni scholars stepped forward for these sciences. They devoted their lives to them, studied them at the hands of their masters, and studied under their luminaries until they excelled and surpassed.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a front matter or introduction from the commentary Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum. The author is explaining the historical phenomenon of Sunni scholars (especially Ash'aris and Hanafis) engaging deeply with rational sciences such as philosophy and logic. He begins by noting that some Indian scholars are considered Ash'ari or Hanafi but follow a different methodology from Taj al-Subki, and this is not unique to India; many prominent Ash'ari imams like al-Razi, al-Baydawi, al-Iji, al-Sa'd, al-Sharif, al-Dawani, and even al-Ghazali engaged in these sciences. The author then poses the question of how to justify this phenomenon. The answer is that these scholars were united on the creed of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari; they studied the sciences of their opponents in order to refute them effectively. They became so proficient that they even became authorities in those sciences, to the point that Avicennan philosophy was understood through their commentaries. However, the author warns of potential negative effects, comparing it to a soldier who, despite being armed with Shari'a sciences, may still be wounded in battle. The passage emphasizes that Sunni scholars stepped forward to study these sciences to demonstrate the superiority of Ahl al-Sunna.

العلوم العقلية
الكلام
الفلسفة

Introduction and Front Matter

The Method of Engaging Foreign Sciences in Da'wa
Author defends Sunni scholars studying opponents' sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rationale behind Sunni scholars studying non-Muslim sciences and identify the two historical examples given in the passage.

Core Idea
Mastering opponents' sciences is a prophetic method to prove Islam's superiority.

This lesson unpacks a key methodological principle in Islamic da'wa: engaging with the sciences of opponents not out of fascination, but to demonstrate the superiority of truth. The author uses a powerful metaphor of a warrior and provides concrete historical examples, including Imam al-Razi's work on music. Understanding this method equips you to see how Islamic scholarship has always met challenges on the intellectual battlefield.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opening metaphor
Find the simile: a warrior carrying weapons into battle, risking injury. This illustrates that studying opponents' sciences is a form of jihad with risks.
2
Extract the rationale for studying foreign sciences
The author states that Sunni scholars aimed to prove the superiority of Ahl al-Sunna in all sciences, especially those boasted about by opponents like philosophers and Mu'tazila. They studied from opponents' teachers to eventually surpass them.
3
Note the two key examples
Imam al-Razi wrote an unmatched book on music; another Sunni scholar wrote an unmatched book on magic. The purpose was to prove genius and superiority, not to teach these arts.
4
Connect to the prophetic method
The author ties this to the method of prophets: Musa challenged magicians with magic, Muhammad challenged with eloquence. Thus, mastering the opponent's strong suit is a sunnah in da'wa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما مثاله إلا كمن يحمل سلاحا يحارب به الكفار، يخوض غمار المعركة، ويقاتل بكل بسالة، إلا أنه قد يصيبه ما شاء الله أن يصيبه، وتتلطخ بالدماء، وقلما ينجو من ذلك. كما أن من أئمتنا من أراد أن يُثبت تفرُّقَ أهل السنة على غيرهم في جميع العلوم، لا سيما العلوم التي يفتخر بها الخصوم، ويعتزون بل ويتكبرون بها، ويُقللون من شأننا ويستهزؤون بنا، كالفلاسفة والمعتزلة والكيميائيين والفيزيائيين وغيرهم. فانبرى لهذه العلوم طائفة من علماء أهل السنة، وكرسوا لها حياتهم، درسوها على أيديهم، وتتلمذوا لأعلامهم، حتى تفوقوا وبرعوا فيها، بل فاقوا أهلَ هذه الفنون، وأثبتوا أن علومكم بالنسبة لنا ليست بشيء يدعو إلى التكبر والغرور، وها نحن قد ملكنا ناصيتها نتصرف فيها حيث نشاء وكيف نشاء. وهكذا صح أن يقال: إن الإمام الرازي صنف في علم الموسيقى كتابا لم يؤلف في هذا الفن كتاب مثله! وصنف فلان آخر من أهل السنة كتابا في السحر لم يؤلف في هذا الفن مثله! كل ذلك لإثبات عبقريته - وبالتالي عبقرية المذهب الذي هو ينتمي إليه - وللبرهنة على تفوقه على أهل الباطل الذين هم أهل هذه الفنون أصالة، لا لتعليم السحر ونشره بين الناس، كما يتوهمه من نزع منه الأدب، وأساء فهم أهله، فبالفعل فقد انخضعت أمام عبقريتهم جباه العلوم الغريبة، وسجدت لجلالهم صفحات التاريخ. وفي ضوء هذه الحقيقة لا حاجة بنا أن ننكر نسبة كتاب في فن السحر مثلا إلى إمام من أئمتنا إن كان قد ثبتت نسبته إليه بالحقيقة. والحق أن هذا يمثل العمود الفقري لمنهج الدعوة؛ لأنه به يتحقق كون الداعي فوق قومه، وهو منهج الأنبياء وأساس فلسفة المعجزة؛ حيث تحدى رسل الله المنكرين من قومه في الأمر الذي كانوا متفقين فيه، كالسحر في قوم موسى عليه السلام، والبلاغة في قوم سيدنا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم. وهكذا بارز المتخصصون من علمائنا في هذه العلوم أربابها الذين تفاخروا به على أهل السنة، فاستطاع أئمتنا - ولايزال - من خلال هذا المنهج أن يقنع كثيرين ممن فُتِنُوا بالفلسفات الغريبة والعلوم الأجنبية، ويردوهم إلى الحق والهدى، ومن لم يهتدوا منهم فقد تهافتوا أمام ضربات أهل السنة القاسية، بالأسلحة والعتاد نفسها التي يملكها هؤلاء، بل بما هو أحسن منها.
And its example is only like one who carries a weapon to fight the disbelievers, plunges into the thick of battle, and fights with all bravery, except that he may be hit by what Allah wills to hit him, and he becomes stained with blood, and rarely does he escape that. Likewise, among our imams is one who wanted to prove the superiority of Ahl al-Sunna over others in all sciences, especially the sciences that the opponents boast about, and are proud, nay, arrogant about, and belittle us and mock us, such as the philosophers, the Mu'tazila, the chemists, the physicists, and others. So a group of Sunni scholars devoted themselves to these sciences, dedicated their lives to them, studied them under their (the opponents') teachers, and learned from their luminaries, until they excelled and became proficient in them, nay, surpassed the people of these arts, and proved that your sciences are nothing for us that would cause arrogance and pride, and behold we have mastered its reins, manipulating it as we wish and how we wish. Thus it is correct to say: Imam al-Razi composed a book on the science of music such that no book like it has been composed in this art! And another scholar of Ahl al-Sunna composed a book on magic such that no book like it has been composed in this art! All that to prove his genius—and consequently the genius of the madhhab he belongs to—and to demonstrate his superiority over the people of falsehood who are the original masters of these arts, not to teach magic and spread it among the people, as imagined by one who lacks etiquette and misunderstands its people. For indeed, the brows of the foreign sciences bowed before their genius, and the pages of history prostrated to their majesty. In light of this fact, we have no need to deny the attribution of a book on the art of magic, for example, to one of our imams if it has been authentically attributed to him. And the truth is that this represents the backbone of the method of da'wa; because through it the da'ee becomes above his people, and it is the method of the prophets and the basis of the philosophy of the miracle; where Allah's messengers challenged the deniers among their people in that matter in which they were unanimous, such as magic in the people of Musa (peace be upon him), and eloquence in the people of our master the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him). Thus the specialists among our scholars confronted the masters of these sciences who boasted about them over Ahl al-Sunna, so our imams were able—and still are—through this method to convince many who were seduced by strange philosophies and foreign sciences, and to return them to truth and guidance, and those who were not guided collapsed before the harsh blows of Ahl al-Sunna, with the same weapons and equipment that those possess, nay, with something better.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a methodological defense of Sunni scholars who studied the sciences of their opponents (philosophy, chemistry, physics, etc.) in order to refute them and demonstrate the superiority of Islam. The author begins with a metaphor: such a scholar is like a warrior who carries weapons to fight, risking injury. He then explains that some Sunni imams aimed to prove the superiority of Ahl al-Sunna in all sciences, especially those that opponents like philosophers and Mu'tazila boast about. A group of Sunni scholars dedicated themselves to learning these sciences from the opponents themselves, eventually surpassing them. The author cites examples: Imam al-Razi wrote an unmatched book on music, and another Sunni scholar wrote an unmatched book on magic. The purpose was not to teach magic but to prove genius and superiority. The author asserts that this approach is the backbone of da'wa: the da'ee must be above his people, following the prophetic method of challenging opponents in areas they excel (e.g., Musa with magic, Muhammad with eloquence). Through this, Sunni scholars convinced many and refuted others with the opponents' own weapons.

Key Terms
منهج الدعوة
العلوم الغريبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) To entertain the caliphs and gain favor.
D) To translate Greek works for preservation.

Key Takeaway
Mastering the opponents' sciences is a prophetic da'wa strategy to establish the truth of Islam and refute falsehood from a position of strength.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
10 min
The Prophetic Method of Confronting Opponents in Their Own Field
Author defends Ash'ari scholars' study of rational sciences as a tool to refute opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the prophetic model of challenging opponents in their area of expertise and explain how it serves as a foundation for da'wa methodology.

Core Idea
The prophets challenged their people on the very ground where they excelled, turning their strength into a proof of truth.

This passage from Bahr al-'Ulum presents a powerful methodological principle: effective da'wa requires engaging opponents on their own turf. The author connects this to the prophetic miracles, where Musa (peace be upon him) confronted the magicians with their own art, and the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) challenged the Arabs in eloquence. This principle underlies the Ash'ari scholars' engagement with rational sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim
Find the sentence that states the prophetic method is the backbone of da'wa. The author says: 'the truth is that this represents the backbone of the methodology of da'wa' – referring to the approach of mastering opponents' sciences.
2
Extract the prophetic examples
Locate the examples of Musa and the Prophet. Musa challenged the magicians in magic; the Prophet challenged in eloquence. Notice that in both cases, the prophet was an outsider to that field but was given divine assistance.
3
Analyze the logical structure
The method works because it turns the opponent's strongest claim against them. If you can beat them at their own game, it proves you have superior knowledge or divine support. This is a form of refutation using their own premises.
This is analogous to the logical principle of 'asking for the same' in jadal.
4
Apply to the Ash'ari context
The author argues that Ash'ari scholars adopted this method: they mastered rational sciences (philosophy, even magic) not to adopt them but to refute their proponents and demonstrate the superiority of Islam.

الإسلام والعلوم العقلية
ولا شك أن هذا الذي فعله هؤلاء الأئمة هو مما أوجبه الله تعالى عليهم ؛ للدفاع عن حرم الإسلام ،وقدسيته وتثبيت قلوب المؤمنين به، فلا يُلامون أبدا، بل جهودهم مشكورة سرمدا. وهذا التاج السبكي، رأينا كيف كان يكره الفلسفة اليونانية والمشائية، ولكن وجدناه يثني على الغزالي والرازي، وكذلك يثني على البيضاوي والآمدي وغيرهم ممن تسربل بعلوم هؤلاء الفلاسفة، وخاض فيها، وأثنى عليهم، وهو الحارس المتيقظ لعقائد المسلمين، لا يترك صغيرة ولا كبيرة تمس كرامتها وصفائها إلا أحصاها، لم يطعن في هؤلاء الأئمة قط .

Islam and the Rational Sciences
And there is no doubt that what these imams did is among the things that God Almighty obligated upon them; to defend the sanctity of Islam and its holiness and to strengthen the hearts of the believers in it. So they are never to be blamed, rather their efforts are forever appreciated. And this is Taj al-Subki – we saw how he disliked Greek and Peripatetic philosophy, but we found him praising al-Ghazali and al-Razi, and likewise he praised al-Baydawi and al-Amidi and others who wrapped themselves in the sciences of these philosophers and delved into them, and he praised them, while he was the vigilant guardian of the beliefs of the Muslims, leaving no small or big matter that touches their dignity and purity without enumerating it; he never attacked these imams.
And the fame of Sultan al-Ulama' 'Izz al-Din ibn 'Abd al-Salam spread as a jurist on the path of the Salaf, and a careful hadith scholar. No two people differ regarding his imamate and great stature. He was asked about his teacher Sayf al-Din al-Amidi, against whom the Mujassima had much criticism, for reasons including his occupation with these sciences. So what was his reply to the question? He said: If a heretic arises to destroy Islam, al-Amidi is designated to refute him in this time. And he is the one who came out in his funeral procession when al-Amidi died, and countless people came out with him.
And what must be noted here is that our imams, the Ash'aris (may God be pleased with them all), did not abandon Ash'arism nor did they accuse it of backwardness after having delved into those sciences filled with the poisons of doubts and deceptive fantasies, until they became imams pointed to with the finger in those arts, and it became very easy for them to boast over their Ash'ari brothers, or to be arrogant towards them, to the point of leaving the school and attacking it and its heritage and its imams. But none of that happened. Rather, each of them remained upon his adherence that he had before entering into these sciences, not only in principles,
25
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a methodological defense of the Ash'ari scholars' engagement with rational and foreign sciences, such as philosophy, music, and even magic. The author argues that these scholars mastered these fields not for their own sake nor to promote them, but to demonstrate Islamic intellectual superiority and to refute the claims of opponents. He provides examples: Imam al-Razi wrote an unmatched book on music, and another Sunni scholar wrote on magic. The purpose was to prove the brilliance of the individual scholar and thereby the superiority of the Sunni school over those who originally claimed these sciences. The author emphasizes that this does not imply endorsement; rather it is a tool for da'wa, following the prophetic method of challenging opponents on their own ground, as Musa (peace be upon him) confronted the magicians with magic, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) challenged the Arabs with eloquence. The author then discusses specific scholars: Taj al-Subki disliked Greek philosophy but praised those who studied it for defensive purposes, and 'Izz al-Din ibn 'Abd al-Salam defended his teacher al-Amidi despite the latter's involvement in these sciences. Crucially, the author asserts that none of these Ash'ari scholars abandoned their Ash'arism or turned against it after mastering these sciences; they remained steadfast in their principles. This counters the misconception that engaging with foreign sciences necessarily leads to deviation.

أشاعرة
فلسفة يونانية

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate

Distinguishing Refutation from Endorsement in Studying Foreign Sciences
Author defends Ash'ari scholars' study of rational sciences as a tool to refute opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between studying a foreign science for the purpose of refuting its claims versus endorsing its worldview, using the Ash'ari tradition as a case study.

Core Idea
Mastering the opponent's science to refute him is not endorsement—it is intellectual jihad.

A common criticism of Ash'ari scholars is that by engaging with Greek philosophy, musicology, or even magic, they compromised Islamic orthodoxy. This lesson examines the author's rebuttal: the intent and outcome determine whether such study is defensive or endorsing. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the permissibility and wisdom of studying any non-Islamic discipline.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accusation
The passage anticipates a misunderstanding: that writing books on music or magic implies promoting these arts. Find where the author addresses this: 'not to teach magic and spread it among the people, as imagined by one who has been stripped of good manners.'
2
Extract the author's defense
The author states the true purpose: 'to prove his brilliance... and to demonstrate his superiority over the people of falsehood.' The purpose is refutation and demonstration of Islam's superiority.
3
Analyze the examples
Consider al-Razi's book on music: it was unmatched in quality, yet al-Razi remained an Ash'ari. The same for the book on magic. The outcome was that the 'foreheads of strange sciences bowed before their brilliance.' The intent was not to promote but to conquer.
4
Apply the distinction to a new case
Imagine a scholar studying Darwinian evolution. Is it defensive if he uses it to point out its flaws and argue for intelligent design? Is it endorsing if he accepts it wholesale? The passage gives criteria: intent (refutation vs. adoption) and effect (strengthening Islam vs. weakening it).

And this is Taj al-Subki – we saw how he disliked Greek and Peripatetic philosophy, but we found him praising al-Ghazali and al-Razi, and likewise he praised al-Baydawi and al-Amidi and others who wrapped themselves in the sciences of these philosophers and delved into them, and he praised them, while he was the vigilant guardian of the beliefs of the Muslims, leaving no small or big matter that touches their dignity and purity without enumerating it; he never attacked these imams.
And the fame of Sultan al-Ulama' 'Izz al-Din ibn 'Abd al-Salam spread as a jurist on the path of the Salaf, and a careful hadith scholar. No two people differ regarding his imamate and great stature. He was asked about his teacher Sayf al-Din al-Amidi, against whom the Mujassima had much criticism, for reasons including his occupation with these sciences. So what was his reply to the question? He said: If a heretic arises to destroy Islam, al-Amidi is designated to refute him in this time. And he is the one who came out in his funeral procession when al-Amidi died, and countless people came out with him.
And what must be noted here is that our imams, the Ash'aris (may God be pleased with them all), did not abandon Ash'arism nor did they accuse it of backwardness after having delved into those sciences filled with the poisons of doubts and deceptive fantasies, until they became imams pointed to with the finger in those arts, and it became very easy for them to boast over their Ash'ari brothers, or to be arrogant towards them, to the point of leaving the school and attacking it and its heritage and its imams. But none of that happened. Rather, each of them remained upon his adherence that he had before entering into these sciences, not only in principles,
25
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a methodological defense of the Ash'ari scholars' engagement with rational and foreign sciences, such as philosophy, music, and even magic. The author argues that these scholars mastered these fields not for their own sake nor to promote them, but to demonstrate Islamic intellectual superiority and to refute the claims of opponents. He provides examples: Imam al-Razi wrote an unmatched book on music, and another Sunni scholar wrote on magic. The purpose was to prove the brilliance of the individual scholar and thereby the superiority of the Sunni school over those who originally claimed these sciences. The author emphasizes that this does not imply endorsement; rather it is a tool for da'wa, following the prophetic method of challenging opponents on their own ground, as Musa (peace be upon him) confronted the magicians with magic, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) challenged the Arabs with eloquence. The author then discusses specific scholars: Taj al-Subki disliked Greek philosophy but praised those who studied it for defensive purposes, and 'Izz al-Din ibn 'Abd al-Salam defended his teacher al-Amidi despite the latter's involvement in these sciences. Crucially, the author asserts that none of these Ash'ari scholars abandoned their Ash'arism or turned against it after mastering these sciences; they remained steadfast in their principles. This counters the misconception that engaging with foreign sciences necessarily leads to deviation.

Key Terms
علوم عقلية
أشاعرة
فلسفة يونانية

Studying a foreign science for the purpose of refutation is a legitimate and even praiseworthy endeavor, as it uses the opponent's own tools to establish the truth of Islam.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
Critique of Philosophy in Ash'ari Kalam
Author defends Ash'ari kalam and praises al-Razi's critique of Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze the author's critique of the relationship between Ash'ari kalam and Peripatetic philosophy.

Core Idea
Ash'ari kalam and falsafa were never allies; al-Razi's 'commentary' was a covert critique.

This passage is a spirited defense of the Ash'ari kalam tradition against the charge of having allied with Peripatetic philosophy. The author marshals historical evidence, particularly the example of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, to argue that Sunni theologians maintained a critical stance toward Greek-derived philosophy. You will learn to dissect this critique by examining its claims, evidence, and rhetorical strategies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Read the passage and find the sentence that states the relationship between Ash'ari kalam and philosophy. The author asserts that kalam was 'never an ally' of Peripatetic philosophy and that 'war has existed' between them.
Look for the Arabic phrase: 'ولم يكن الكلام الأشعري حليفا للفلسفة المشائية أبدا'
2
Extract the chief evidence
The author uses a direct quote from Imam al-Razi to support the claim. Identify al-Razi's reasons for refusing to comment on Ibn Sina's 'Uyun al-Hikma. He mentions opposition to the book, fear of flattery or abuse, and the discrepancy between the book's size and reputation.
Al-Razi's statement begins with 'أحجمت عنه لأمور...'
3
Analyze the rhetorical strategy
Notice how the author contrasts al-Razi's 'commentary' (sharh) with its true nature as a critique (jarh). The author also invokes al-Tusi's reaction to al-Razi's Muhassal as another example of a 'commentary' being a refutation. This pattern strengthens the argument that Ash'ari scholars did not simply accept philosophy.
The phrase 'ليس شرحا بل كان جرحا' is key.
4
Evaluate the critique's validity
Assess whether the evidence sufficiently proves that Ash'ari kalam never aligned with philosophy. Consider that al-Razi himself used philosophical methods, but the author argues that this was a weapon against philosophy, not an embrace.
This step moves from description to critical assessment.

Commentary
This passage is a historical and methodological defense of the Ash'ari kalam tradition against peripatetic philosophy, presenting key figures and their stance. The author begins by praising the orthodoxy of earlier scholars like al-Razi and al-Amidi in both theology and jurisprudence, attributing their success to divine grace, the fulfillment of conditions noted by al-Taj al-Subki, and their sincere intention to serve Islam. Then, the author asserts that Ash'ari kalam was never allied with Peripatetic philosophy (falsafa) and that Sunni dialectical theologians never accepted the philosophical teachings of works like al-Shifa and al-Isharat; rather, there has been a continuous conflict between the two methodologies. To illustrate this, the author quotes Imam al-Razi's explanation for hesitating to write a commentary on Ibn Sina's 'Uyun al-Hikma: al-Razi states that he disagrees with the book in both its subtle and obvious matters and that engaging with it would either require flattery (implying agreement) or open criticism (leading to abuse). He also notes that the book's small size belies its renown. The author then praises al-Razi, calling him the Imam of Ahl al-Sunna, and remarks that attacks on him only reflect the ignorance of his attackers. The author goes on to highlight al-Razi's role in refuting Ibn Sina, specifically his book that was nominally a 'commentary' on al-Isharat but actually constituted a 'critique' (jarh). This is confirmed by the fact that Nasir al-Din al-Tusi, who opposed al-Razi, also engaged with his legacy by writing a work that was called a 'commentary' on al-Razi's Muhassal but was in reality a 'critique' (Naqid al-Muhassal). The author then laments the current situation where students of English tend to deviate into various sects or even apostasy, but clarifies that this is not a call to close the doors of knowledge, but rather a warning about the dangers of studying without proper grounding. The passage is rich in polemical language and underscores the perceived threat of philosophical thinking to Islamic orthodoxy.

Key Terms
الكلام الأشعري (al-kalam al-Ash'ari): The dialectical theology of the Ash'ari school, which the author defends as being in opposition to Peripatetic philosophy. [Note: No significant shift; stable across the tradition.]
الفلسفة المشائية (al-falsafa al-masha'iyya): Peripatetic philosophy, the Aristotelian tradition followed by Ibn Sina and others, which the author argues is fundamentally opposed to Ash'ari kalam. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable.]
الإمام الرازي (al-Imam al-Razi): Fakhr al-Din al-Razi (1149–1209), a prominent Ash'ari theologian and critic of Ibn Sina, whose works are presented as critiques disguised as commentaries. [Note: Al-Razi is often seen as a key figure in the integration of philosophy into kalam, but here he is portrayed as an opponent.]

C) A summary of Ibn Sina's philosophy.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that Ash'ari kalam never allied with Peripatetic philosophy, and al-Razi's so-called commentary was actually a critique, exemplifying the adversarial relationship between the two traditions.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Prerequisites for Studying Foreign Sciences
Author argues for necessary conditions before engaging with foreign sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the essential prerequisites that a student of foreign sciences must possess according to the Sunni scholarly tradition, as articulated in this passage.

Core Idea
Sound knowledge begins with a sound creed – no intellectual exploration is safe without Ash'ari foundations.

This passage is a methodological preface from a logic textbook. The author insists that studying foreign sciences like philosophy or logic requires intellectual and creedal preparation. He warns against the historical and contemporary dangers of engaging with such knowledge without a firm grounding in orthodox Sunni theology and jurisprudence. The goal is to equip the student to engage critically without drifting from the path of Ahl al-Sunna.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main concern
Read the passage and pinpoint what the author sees as the primary danger of studying foreign sciences without preparation. He mentions historical deviations and contemporary examples of those who lost their faith after exposure to new ideas.
2
Extract the historical examples
Note the cases of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's engagement with Ibn Sina, and Nasir al-Din al-Tusi's work on al-Razi's Muhassal. Understand that these were not neutral commentaries but critical refutations, showing how scholars engaged with foreign sciences while defending orthodoxy.
3
Pinpoint the essential condition
Find the sentence where the author states the only person who can safely engage with new sciences: one who holds the creed of al-Ash'ari and follows one of the four Sunni schools. This is the core prerequisite.
4
Connect to the Qur'anic directive
The author cites the first revealed verse 'Iqra' bismi rabbika' to argue that all reading must be done 'in the name of your Lord'. Explain how this implies that knowledge acquisition must be anchored in divine consciousness and the recognition of human limitation.
5
Summarize the prerequisites
Based on the passage, list the conditions: firm Ash'ari creed, adherence to a Sunni madhhab, awareness of divine sovereignty, and the ability to engage in dialogue without being dazzled. Note the author's rejection of both isolationist ignorance and uncritical openness.

من هنا لا يزال دعاة الفكر الفلسفي حربا على أئمتنا طوال التاريخ، وهل يخفى علينا دور الفخر الرازي نفسه ذلك الذي يهاجمه الحشوية من كل جانب، وهل يخفى دوره في إشعال نار هذه المعركة ؟ وقد تصدى لابن سينا بعقليته النافذة، فجاء بكتاب ينقض ما أثبته ابن سينا في إشاراته، وإن سماه الرازي شرحا ، إلا أنه لم يكن شرحا ، بل كان جرحا ـ على لسان نصير دينهم الطوسي ووزيرهم الغنوصي ـ ثم لما ذا انعطفت جهود الطوسي على تراث فخر ديننا الرازي واحدا تلو الآخر؟ لما ألف كتابا حول «محصل» الرازي، وإن سماه الطوسي تلخيص المحصل، أو سماه البعض «شرح المحصل»، إلا أنه في الحقيقة «نقد المحصل، وليس ذلك خافيا على ذي البصر والبصيرة، ويطلق عليه المحققون في كتبهم ناقد المحصل ، فلما ذا إذن ؟ ولكن أين نحن اليوم ؟ يتصدى بعضنا لدراسة أبجديات اللغة الإنجليزية مثلا ، فيضل الطريق، يصير إما شيعيا أو معتزليا أو وهابيا أو علمانيا لبراليا، بل إن شئت قلت يخلع رقبة الإسلام من عنقه، فيصير مرتدا كافرا، عياذا بالله ، فضلا عن الانحراف الكثير الذي يصيب كبار المثقفين. ولسنا نُغلق أبوابَ العلم والمعرفة، ولا ندعو للتجاهل عما يحدث فيما حولنا، وهذا بلا شك موقف سلبي يتسارع الجبان إلى التبري عنه، فكيف بالذي ينتمي إلى فلسفة الحوار وفكر المحاججة ! ؟ بل إن كان هناك فلسفة أو دين أو دستور حياةٍ لا يخاف المواجهة والحوار فهو الإسلام المتمثل في مذاهب أهل السنة فقط ، وأن الوحيد الذي أظهر - ولا يزال - حرصه على المغامرة، ولم يخش ركوب الأخطار والأهوال هو الرجل الذي يعتقد اعتقاد الأشعري، ويتمذهب بواحد من المذاهب الأربعة ؛ لأنه الوحيد الذي يملك الحجة ويهديه نور المحجة. وإنما الذي قصدته من خلال هذا الكلام هو أن الاطلاع على الجديد من العلوم الغريبة والثقافات الأجنبية لا بد وأن تسبقه شروط وشروط ؛ كما صرح به المحققون من الأئمة، حتى لا يضل بصاحبه ولا يُغويه. وهذه الشروط عبارة عن الحالة أو الملكة التي تجعل الإنسان لا ينحرف عن منهج أهل السنة والجماعة قيد أنملة، وتجعله يصمد أمام الاكتشافات الجديدة، ولا ينبهر بمجرد ما يسمع عن جديد أو يطلع على ما هو غريب عنده . ويمكننا استخراج نصوص من القرآن والسنة تشير إلى ضرورة توفر هذه الشروط فيمن يتصدى لقراءة الجديد وكشف الغريب ، لما ذا نذهب بعيدا ؛ فإن أول توجيه إلهي للبشرية ينطوي على هذا الاشتراط وأهميته ؛ حيث قال: بِاسْمِ رَبِّكَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ [العلق: ۱] . فالقراءة بدون استحضار معنى الرب، وبدون اعتبار القدرة المطلقة للخالق ، ومحدودية الكائن المخلوق لا تكون قراءة بناءة أبدا . وقد أمرنا النبي صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ بأن نكلم الناس بقدر عقولهم. ثم إنه يوجد في تراثنا العقلي أساليب وطرق قد لا تعجبنا أحيانا، مثل المجاراة والتنزل لمخالفي السنة والدين، وكذكر رؤوس تلك العلوم ورؤساء تلك الفرق بالإجلال والتعظيم، فلا يريح ذلك كثيرا من طلبة العلم؛ لعدم الاطلاع على أسرار الحوار .
From here, the proponents of philosophical thought have not ceased to be at war with our imams throughout history. And is it hidden from us the role of Fakhr al-Razi himself, the one whom the Hashwiyya attack from every side, and is his role in igniting the fire of this battle hidden? He confronted Ibn Sina with his penetrating intellect, and produced a book that refutes what Ibn Sina established in his Isharat, even though al-Razi called it a commentary, but it was not a commentary; rather it was a wound—according to Nasir al-Din al-Tusi, the supporter of their religion and their gnostic vizier. Then why did al-Tusi's efforts turn to the legacy of the pride of our religion al-Razi one after another? Why did he write a book about al-Razi's Muhassal, and although al-Tusi called it Talkhis al-Muhassal, or some called it Sharh al-Muhassal, it is in reality Naqd al-Muhassal, and that is not hidden from one with insight and discernment, and the researchers refer to it in their books as Naqid al-Muhassal. So why then? But where are we today? Some of us undertake to study the ABCs of the English language, for example, and then go astray, becoming either a Shi'i, a Mu'tazili, a Wahhabi, a secular liberal, or—if you will—one who removes the collar of Islam from his neck, becoming an apostate infidel, God forbid, besides the great deviation that afflicts senior intellectuals. We do not close the doors of knowledge and learning, nor do we call for ignoring what is happening around us; this is undoubtedly a negative position that the coward hastens to disavow. So how about one who belongs to the philosophy of dialogue and the thought of disputation? Indeed, if there is any philosophy, religion, or constitution of life that does not fear confrontation and dialogue, it is Islam represented in the schools of Ahl al-Sunna only. And the only one who has shown—and still shows—eagerness for adventure and does not fear riding dangers and terrors is the man who holds the creed of al-Ash'ari and follows one of the four schools, because he is the only one who possesses the proof and is guided by the light of the path. What I intended through this speech is that examining new foreign sciences and cultures must be preceded by conditions upon conditions, as stated by the verifying imams, so that it does not lead its possessor astray or seduce him. These conditions are the state or disposition that prevents a person from deviating from the methodology of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a by even a fingertip, and makes him withstand new discoveries, not being dazzled by merely hearing something new or seeing what is strange to him. We can extract texts from the Qur'an and Sunna indicating the necessity of these conditions being present in one who undertakes to read the new and uncover the strange. Why go far? The first divine directive to humanity includes this condition and its importance, where He said: 'In the name of your Lord who created' [al-'Alaq: 1]. So reading without invoking the meaning of the Lord, and without considering the absolute power of the Creator and the limitation of the created being, will never be constructive reading. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, commanded us to speak to people according to the capacity of their intellects. Then indeed, in our intellectual legacy there are methods and ways that may sometimes not please us, such as accommodating and descending to the level of those who oppose the Sunna and religion, and mentioning the heads of those sciences and the leaders of those sects with veneration and exaltation. That does not please many students of knowledge because they are unaware of the secrets of dialogue.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is an introductory discussion by the author, setting the stage for the study of logic (ʿIlm al-Manṭiq) by emphasizing the intellectual and creedal prerequisites. The author begins by noting the historical hostility of philosophers towards the Sunni imams, citing Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's critical engagement with Ibn Sina as an example. Al-Razi's commentary on Ibn Sina's Isharat was actually a refutation. Similarly, Nasir al-Din al-Tusi's 'commentary' on al-Razi's Muhassal was also a critique. The author then turns to the present day, lamenting that some who study foreign languages or sciences abandon their Islamic identity, becoming deviant sects or even apostates. He clarifies that he does not reject knowledge; rather, he advocates for proper preparation. The key condition is a firm grounding in Ash'ari creed and adherence to one of the four Sunni schools. Only such a person can engage with new ideas without being misled. The author supports this by citing the Qur'anic command to read in the name of the Lord, implying that all reading must be done with consciousness of the Creator. He also invokes the prophetic principle of speaking according to people's intellectual capacities. Finally, he notes that Islamic intellectual tradition contains strategies like condescension in debate that may appear problematic to the uninformed but are rooted in wisdom. This passage serves as a methodological preface, underscoring that the study of logic must be anchored in orthodox Sunni theology.

Prerequisites for Studying Foreign Sciences
Author discusses necessary conditions for studying foreign sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the prerequisites for studying foreign sciences according to the author and explain why a fixed disposition (malakah) in Sunni orthodoxy is necessary.

Core Idea
Engaging with foreign sciences requires a prior disposition rooted in Sunni creed.

The author, writing as a commentator on 'Sullam al-Ulum', addresses a critical methodological question: under what conditions may a Muslim scholar or student safely engage with foreign sciences? He argues that Islam itself does not fear dialogue, but the individual must be equipped with a strong epistemological foundation. In this lesson, we will unpack the conditions he prescribes and understand why he considers them essential.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's core claim

2
Extract the concept of malakah
Find the definition the author gives for the required state: a disposition that prevents deviation from Ahl al-Sunnah methodology and enables resilience against new discoveries.
The term 'الملكة' is introduced as the central condition.
3
Connect malakah to the Quranic example
The author cites the first revealed verse, 'Read in the name of your Lord', to show that reading must be done with consciousness of the Lord and the Creator's absolute power. This illustrates how malakah is operationalized: every intellectual engagement must be framed by tawhid.
The verse from Surah al-Alaq is used as a foundational proof.
4
Understand the author's defense of accommodating methods
The author acknowledges that some methods in Islamic intellectual heritage (e.g., using opponents' terminology, showing respect to their leaders) may seem uncomfortable. He defends them by citing the Prophet's conduct at Hudaybiyyah and scholars' use of 'Proving the Maker of the world' instead of 'Proving God'.
This shows that malakah also includes the wisdom to adapt language without compromising doctrine.

Philosophy's Influence on Arabic Grammar: The Basran School
Author describes how Greek logic shaped the Basran grammatical school.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the evidence the author uses to support the claim that the Basran grammatical school was shaped by Greek logic.

Core Idea
Greek logic didn't just shape Islamic philosophy—it shaped Arabic grammar itself.

This passage challenges the misconception that philosophy only influenced abstract theology. The author shows that rational, systematic thinking entered even the field of Arabic grammar, transforming the Basran school into a distinctly 'logical' school. We will trace the author's argument and see how he connects philosophical influence to the origins of language debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim
What is the central thesis about the Basran school? Look for the sentence that states it became a 'logical school' distinct from the Kufan school.
2
Find the evidence for philosophical influence in Basra
Which groups does the author name as carriers of foreign wisdom? Check for mention of Shiites and Mu'tazilites and their role in paving the way.
3
Trace the connection to the origin-of-language debate
How does the author link the dispute between tawqīf (divine decree) and waḍʿ (convention) to philosophical influence? Look at the final sentence.
4
Evaluate the author's use of attribution
Does the author successfully defend philosophy from the charge of being the source of false doctrines? Consider his opening remarks about the eternity of the world and God's knowledge of particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جزء من الفلسفة، وليس آلة لها فحسب. ونسبة كثير من الأقوال الباطلة - كقدم العالم نفسه - إليه ليست محل إجماع، بل من الباحثين من يرى من المليين لم يبلغ في العلم بحدوثه ما بأن أحدا أكّد على حدوثه، وصرح بلغه أفلاطون وأرسطو، وكذا الأمر بالنسبة لعلم الله بالجزئيات. والحق أن النشاط العقلي لم يكن قاصرا على الفلسفة والمنطلق فقط، بل وصل الأمر حتى علم اللغة، وصار تيار بأسره - وهو تيار البصريين - تيارا منطقيا، يتميز عن المذهب الكوفي، ولا يخفى على المطلع أن تأثير المذاهب الفلسفية ظهر في البصرة قبل ظهوره في غيرها، وكان بين نحاة البصرة كثير من الشيعة والمعتزلة، الذين أفسحوا السبيل للحكمة الأجنبية. ولا يخفى على المطلع أيضا الاختلاف حول أصل نشأة اللغة، هل هي توقيف أم وضع واضع، ذلك النزاع الذي يمتد جذوره إلى الأثر الفلسفي.
Part of philosophy, and not merely a tool for it. And attributing many false statements - such as the eternity of the world itself - to it is not a matter of consensus; rather, some researchers from the religious scholars hold that no one reached in knowledge of its temporal origination what Plato and Aristotle affirmed, and likewise regarding God's knowledge of particulars. The truth is that intellectual activity was not limited to philosophy and logic only, but it extended even to the science of language, and an entire school - namely the Basran school - became a logical school, distinct from the Kufan school. And it is not hidden from the observer that the influence of philosophical schools appeared in Basra before it appeared elsewhere, and among the Basran grammarians were many Shiites and Mu'tazilites, who paved the way for foreign wisdom. And it is also not hidden from the observer the disagreement about the origin of language: whether it is by divine decree or by convention of an establisher, a debate whose roots extend to philosophical influence.

Commentary
This passage is a historical and methodological introduction to the relationship between Greek philosophy and Islamic sciences. The author first addresses a common misconception: that philosophy is the source of false doctrines like the eternity of the world or denial of God's knowledge of particulars. He notes that this attribution is not universally accepted; some Muslim scholars deny that ancient philosophers held those views unequivocally. The main point is that rational inquiry ('al-nashāt al-'aqlī) was not confined to philosophy and logic but also permeated linguistics. The Basran school of Arabic grammar (nahw) became a 'logical school' characterized by systematic reasoning, in contrast to the more literal Kufan school. The author attributes this to the prevalence of philosophical ideas in Basra, reinforced by the presence of Shiites and Mu'tazilites who were more open to foreign wisdom. Finally, he highlights the classic debate on the origin of language (al-tawqīf vs. al-waḍʿ), which he says is rooted in philosophical discourse. This passage shows how logic and philosophy shaped even the study of Arabic grammar.

Logic's Integration into Usul al-Fiqh
Author discusses impact of logic on usul al-fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify two major works of usul al-fiqh that explicitly integrate logical discussions and explain why qiyas depends on logic.

Core Idea
Usul al-fiqh is saturated with logic; half of fiqh is qiyas, and qiyas is built on logical foundations.

This lesson explores a historical claim: that usul al-fiqh, the methodology of Islamic jurisprudence, is deeply infused with logical reasoning. The author points to the introductions of al-Ghazali's al-Mustasfa and Ibn al-Hajib's Mukhtasar al-Muntaha as prime examples. Even more fundamentally, the very structure of qiyas (analogical reasoning) depends on precise logical categories and inference rules.

Guided Path
1

3
Analyze the connection between qiyas and logic
The author says half of fiqh is qiyas, and qiyas developed by usul scholars relies on the finest details of logic. Consider why this connection is essential for the validity of legal analogy.
4
Reflect on the implications
What does this tell you about the relationship between Islamic jurisprudence and logic? How does this affect how you study usul al-fiqh?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحال علم الأصول - أصول الفقه - لا تختلف عما ذكرنا، وقد امتلأت بطون كتب الأصول بالآراء والمباحث المتعلقة بالعلوم العقلية والمنطقية، ومن أبرز الأمثلة على ذلك مقدمة «المستصفى» للإمام الغزالي ومقدمة «مختصر المنتهى الأصولي» لابن الحاجب. كما أن الفقه أيضا ما استطاع أن ينفلت من قبضة هذا التأثر، وكيف يمكنه ذلك، ونصفُ الفقه القياس، إن لم يكن كله، والقياس ـ في الصورة التي تطور إليها على أيدي علماء الأصول ـ يعتمد على أدق تفاصيل المنطق والفلسفة. ثم للإمام فخر الدين الرازي دور كبير في تطوير العلوم العقلية: فلسفة ومنطقا وكلاما وهو صاحب مدرسة فيها تتميز بمميزات وخصائص، جرى على إثره أمة من أهل العلم كالبيضاوي والأرموي والإيجي، كما لقي بعض آرائه واتجاهاته نقدا ورفضا من قبل طائفة أخرى. وما تركه الرازي من المؤلفات، كـ «الملخص و المحصل» و«المباحث المشرقية» ونهاية العقول» و شرح الإشارات» وغيرها يمثل مرحلة فارقة في طريق التطور.
The condition of the science of principles - usul al-fiqh - is no different from what we mentioned. The volumes of usul books are filled with opinions and investigations related to rational and logical sciences. Among the most prominent examples are the introduction of 'al-Mustasfa' by Imam al-Ghazali and the introduction of 'Mukhtasar al-Muntaha al-Usuli' by Ibn al-Hajib. Likewise, fiqh itself could not escape the grip of this influence; how could it, when half of fiqh is qiyas (analogy), if not all of it, and qiyas - in the form it developed in the hands of usul scholars - depends on the finest details of logic and philosophy. Then Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi played a great role in developing the rational sciences: philosophy, logic, and kalam. He was the founder of a school with distinct characteristics, followed by a multitude of scholars such as al-Baydawi, al-Urmawi, and al-Iji. Some of his views and orientations were criticized and rejected by another group. The works he left, such as 'al-Mulakhkhas', 'al-Muhassal', 'al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya', 'Nihayat al-Uqul', 'Sharh al-Isharat', and others, represent a turning point in the path of development.

Commentary
This passage continues the historical narrative, now focusing on usul al-fiqh and the pivotal role of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. The author states that usul al-fiqh is saturated with rational and logical discussions, citing the introductions of al-Ghazali's al-Mustasfa and Ibn al-Hajib's Mukhtasar al-Muntaha as examples. Fiqh itself relies heavily on qiyas (analogical reasoning), and the refined form of qiyas developed by usul scholars is deeply grounded in logic and philosophy. The author then highlights al-Razi's contributions: he advanced philosophy, logic, and kalam, establishing a school followed by notable figures like al-Baydawi, al-Urmawi, and al-Iji, though also faced criticism. Al-Razi's major works (listed) marked a transformative stage in the integration of rational sciences into Islamic thought. This passage underscores that logic is not an alien discipline but integral to the core Islamic sciences.
